<p>Engineering Drawing
Module 7. Key, cotter joints and pin joints</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-11">Lesson 11.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-11" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-key-cotter-joints-and-pin-joints">Types of Key, Cotter joints and Pin joints<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-key-cotter-joints-and-pin-joints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Keys, cotter-joints and pin-joints are employed as removable/ temporary fasteners.</li>
<li>Keys are also employed to secure gears, pulleys, discs, flange and other similar parts to shafts or axles.</li>
<li>Cotter-joints and pin joints are used for rods in tensile or compressive load.</li>
<li>The cotter-joint does not permit any axial mis-alignment as it is the rigid-joint.</li>
<li>The pin-joint permits angular mis-alignment as it is flexible-joint.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="key-joints">Key Joints<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#key-joints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A key is employed to connect two pieces, like shaft and a gear, in such a way that there is no relative rotational movement between the shaft and the parts mounted on it. Key is always made of steel because it is subjected to shearing and torsional stresses. It is inserted in the axial direction between the shaft and the boss or hub of the mating piece. A keyway is the groove cut in the shaft as well as in the hub, to accommodate the key. Fig.11.1.1 shows the parts of key joints and its assembly.</p>
<p>Keys may be classified as follows:</p>
<p>(i) taper keys and (ii) parallel or feather keys.</p>
<p>(i) Taper keys : It is tapered (standard taper of this key is 1 in 100) in thickness but uniform in width. A taper key joint prevents relative rotational as well as axial movement between the two pieces. The types of taper keys are; (1) Sunk taper key, which is a standard form of key and may be either of square or rectangular cross-section. Half part of its nominal thickness is sunk in the shaft key way and the remaining half portion fits in the keyway inside the hub or boss of the mating piece. The depth of the keyway is tapering in the hub but uniform inside the shaft (fig. 11.1.1), (2) Saddle keys are also made in two forms i.e. hollow and flat. A hollow saddle key has its underside hollow to fit the curved surface of the shaft. A flat saddle key inserted on a flat surface provided on the shaft. (3) Round key (pin key) is of circular cross-section, usually tapered along the length and is inserted in a hole drilled partly in the shaft and partly in the hub of mating piece. (4) Gib-head is a taper key is usually provided with a head, called a gib-head, to facilitate its removal.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-70ac0483.jpeg" alt="img-245.jpeg">
Fig. 11.1.1. Sunk taper key joint</p>
<p>(ii) Parallel or feather keys: When there is a requirement of sliding or axial movement of mating piece on the shaft, a feather key is used. It is a sunk key of uniform width and thickness. It may be of various cross sections like rectangular, square or dove-tail. This key may be secured to either the shaft by means of two cap-screws, having countersunk heads or the mating piece and free to slide in the keyways in the shaft. These keys are also classified as peg feather key, single headed and double headed feather key.</p>
<p>Woodruf key: This is a sunk key in the form of a segmental part of a circular disc having uniform thickness. It is inserted into a corresponding form of a circular keyway cut in the shaft so that the some portion projects outside. This projecting part fits in a keyway inside the mating piece of the comparatively greater depth of the keyway cut in it. Once placed in position, the key tilts align it self on the tapered shaft.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cotter-joints">Cotter Joints :<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cotter-joints" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A cotter is a flat wedge-shaped piece of rectangular cross section and made of steel. It is used to connect two rods rigidly subjected to compressive or tensile forces and is inserted at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>90</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">90^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">9</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> to the axes of the rods. It is tapering in width, generally on one side only but uniform in thickness. The bearing edge of the cotter and the bearing slots are generally made semicircular. This joint is not suitable for</p>
<p>connecting the rotating members. The most commonly used types of cotter joints are; (i) Cotter joint with Socket and spigot end (fig. 11.1.2): A socket is formed by expanding the end of one rod in which the spigot-end of the other rod is inserted. A cotter is driven tightly through the slots (in the rods) which are kept slightly mis aligned. The cotter comes in contact with the two rods on opposite sides and leaves clearances on both sides. Clearances are absolutely required for proper functioning of the joint. (ii) Sleeve cotter joint: This is the simplest form of cotter joint used for fastening the two circular rods. The ends of the two rods are inserted from opposite sides in a cylindrical sleeve or steel box. Two cotters are inserted through the slots on each rod end. The slots in the rods and sleeve are made slightly wider than the width of cotter.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-a170b1f1.jpeg" alt="img-246.jpeg">
Fig 11.1.2 Cotter joint with socket and spigot ends</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pin-joint-or-knuckle-joint">Pin-Joint or Knuckle Joint:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pin-joint-or-knuckle-joint" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The joint is used for rods whose axes intersect i.e which may not be in alignment. It is employed in tension or compression. The joint permits angular movement between the rods there fore it is not rigid. One end of a rod is formed in a single-eye end and is placed within a double-eye end or</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>forked end of the other rod (fig. 11.1.3). A cylindrical pin is inserted through holes of single and double eye. The pin is kept in position by means of a collar and a taper pin. The rods are quite free to swivel on the cylindrical pin.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-f09d12f3.jpeg" alt="img-247.jpeg">
(a)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-f3625330.jpeg" alt="img-248.jpeg">
(b)
Fig 11.1.3 Knuckle (pin) joint</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing
Module 8. Shaft Couplings</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-12">Lesson 12.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-12" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rigid-coupling-loose-coupling-flexible-coupling-universal-coupling">Rigid coupling, Loose coupling, Flexible coupling, Universal coupling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rigid-coupling-loose-coupling-flexible-coupling-universal-coupling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Shaft couplings are used for connecting two shafts end for transmitting rotary motion directly from one shaft to another. They act as a one unit and transmit power. The classification of shaft coupling is as below:</p>
<p>A. Rigid couplings
B. Flexible couplings and
C. Loose couplings</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-rigid-coupling">(A) Rigid Coupling:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-rigid-coupling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It does not permit any relative rotation between the two shafts i.e two coupled shaft end work as a rigid piece. It is used for connecting the shaft having collinear axis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="muff-coupling">Muff coupling:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#muff-coupling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It consists a sleeve (muff) of cast iron fitted over the ends of the shaft which are to be connected. The sunk key is inserted in the key ways in the shafts and sleeve. The types of rigid couplings are (1) Box or Butt- muff coupling (fig. 12.1.1) in which two shaft's ends butt against each other, (2) Half-lap coupling in which the shaft's ends are tapered and overlap each other for short length (3) Split-muff coupling in which the muff is split in two semi-cylindrical halves which are joined together by means of bolts and nuts. The two halves are recessed in such a way that they can accommodate the required number of nuts and bolt-heads.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="flanged-coupling">Flanged coupling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#flanged-coupling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is an extensively used and standard form of coupling. Two cast-iron flanges are either fitted or provided at the ends of the two shafts and fastened together by means of a number of bolts and nuts (fig. 12.1.2). Sunk taper keys of rectangular or square cross-section are commonly used in this. The types of flange couplings are; (1) Protected type flange-coupling in which flange is provided with a shroud or annular projection, which shelters or covers the bolt-heads or the nuts, and prevents them from catching clothes of workmen, thus it provides the safety to the worker (2) Solid flanged coupling in which flanges are forged solid integral with the both shafts</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5656dec6.jpeg" alt="img-249.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-723aa3f2.jpeg" alt="img-250.jpeg">
Fig.12.1.1 Box of butt muff coupling</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-c432e368.jpeg" alt="img-251.jpeg"></p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-15604362.jpeg" alt="img-252.jpeg">
(a)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-5420502e.jpeg" alt="img-253.jpeg">
Fig.12.1.2 Flanged coupling</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="b-flexible-couplings">(B) Flexible Couplings<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-flexible-couplings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A flexible coupling permits relative rotation and variation in the alignment of shafts axes within some limit. The types of flexible couplings are; (1) Bushed Pin type flanged coupling which is a modified version of a protected flange coupling in which bolts are replaced by bushed pins fastened one of the flange covered with leather or rubber washer and kept loose in other flange. (2) Universal coupling (fig. 12.1.3) is used to couple two shafts whose axes intersect if extended. Two same forks are keyed on to the ends of the two shafts. These forks are pinjointed in to a centre-block having two arms at right angles to one another in the form of a cross. The angle between the shafts may be varied even during its rotary motion. (3) Oldham's coupling (fig. 12.1.4) is used to connect two parallel shafts whose axes are at a small distance apart and not in alignment. Two flanges, with a rectangular recess on each, are keyed, one on each shaft. Another piece in the form of a circular disc with two rectangular projecting parts at right angles to one another on its opposite sides and, is placed between the two flanges.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-1b83eb67.jpeg" alt="img-254.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-684844d5.jpeg" alt="img-255.jpeg">
Fig. 12.1.3 Universal coupling</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-90d3834d.jpeg" alt="img-256.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-fb384b05.jpeg" alt="img-257.jpeg">
(b)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-70306c16.jpeg" alt="img-258.jpeg">
Fig.12.1.4 Oldham coupling</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="c-loose-couplings">(C)Loose Couplings:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c-loose-couplings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>With the help of this type of coupling, the shafts may be coupled together or disengaged when required, even while they may be in rotary motion. It consists one part rigidly fixed to the driving shaft and other parts mounted with provision for sliding over the driven shaft for engaging and disengaging. The examples of loose couplings are (1) Claw coupling (fig. 12.1.5) which is a disengaging type of coupling, generally used for slow - speed shafts. One flange is rigidly attached to the end of one shaft by a sunk taper key while other flange is keyed to the other shaft by a feather key so that it can slide freely on that shaft. Each flange has a number of identical projecting claws which engage/fit into the corresponding recesses in the other flange on other shaft. Sliding flange has a groove in the boss in which the forked end of a lever fits. With the help of this lever this flange is made to slide so as to engage with or disengage from the first flange. (2) Cone coupling (fig. 12.1.6) which consists of male cone, female cone, two keys driving and driven shaft. This is</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>operated in the same manner as the claw coupling. The shafts are coupled by virtue of friction between the two conical surfaces of the flanges.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-6e955c14.jpeg" alt="img-259.jpeg">
Fig.12.1.5 Claw coupling</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-c84b3f27.jpeg" alt="img-260.jpeg">
Fig. 12.1.6 Cone coupling</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p>Engineering Drawing
Module 9. Types of bearings</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-13">Lesson 13.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-13" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="shaft-bearing-journal-bearings-pivot-bearings-collar-bearings">Shaft bearing: Journal bearings, Pivot bearings, Collar bearings<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shaft-bearing-journal-bearings-pivot-bearings-collar-bearings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bearing provide supports/stability for rotating members/shaft as well as permit free and smooth motion. Bearing are classified according to the direction of pressure with respect to the axis of the shaft. Journal bearings, Pivot bearings and Collar bearings are sliding contact type bearing in which the rotating shaft has sliding contact with the bearing and the friction between two is relatively high.</p>
<p><strong>Journal Bearings:</strong> The load on the bearing is perpendicular to the axis of the shaft. Solid, bushed and pedestal are the types of journal bearing. In solid and bushed bearings, the shaft can be inserted endwise only. Hence, they are generally placed only at or near the ends of the shaft.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solid-bearing">Solid bearing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solid-bearing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This simplest form of journal bearing is made of cast-iron and consists a cylindrical block with a rectangular base [fig.13.1.1].</li>
<li>A hole is bored in the cylindrical part of the bearing of the size equal to the diameter of the shaft.</li>
<li>Rectangular base-plate has two holes drilled in it for bolting down the bearing in its position.</li>
<li>An oil-hole to lubricate the bearing is drilled at the top. Friction between the bearing and the shaft is thus reduced.</li>
<li>There is no any provision for adjustment for wear. Therefore, it is mostly used for shafts which rotate at comparatively slow speeds, carry light loads and the wear is immaterial.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bushed-bearing">Bushed bearing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bushed-bearing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Bushed bearing is a somewhat modified version of solid bearing, consisting mainly two parts, the body and the bush [fig.13.1.2].</li>
<li>It is lined with a hollow bush of gun-metal or brass. The bush is pressed inside the bore in the bearing and is checked from sliding or rotating by means of a dowel-pin or grub-screw inserted half inside the block and half in the bush.</li>
<li>For lubrication of the bearing, a oil hole is provided at the top of the body</li>
</ul>
<p>When the bush wear out it is easily removed and replaced by a new one.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-12385021.jpeg" alt="img-261.jpeg">
Fig.13.1.1 Solid journal bearing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-2cd35524.jpeg" alt="img-262.jpeg">
Fig.13.1.2 Bushed bearing</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pedestal-bearing-plummer-block">Pedestal bearing (plummer block)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pedestal-bearing-plummer-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>A pedestal bearing consists of cast-iron pedestal or block, gun-metal brasses made in two halves, a cast-iron cap and two mild-steel bolts [fig. 13.1.3].</li>
<li>It is made in two halves for placing and removal of the shaft in and from the bearings, for adjustment of wear in the brasses and for renewal of brasses.</li>
<li>The cap while resting on the upper step fits inside the block at its sides but does not sit on it.</li>
<li>The rotary motion motion of the bush is checked by snug provided at the bottom of the lower brass.</li>
<li>Two square-headed bolts are used to fasten the cap and the block together. The square heads fit in square recesses at the bottom of the pedestal and prevent rotation of the bolts.</li>
</ul>
<p>Pivot Bearing: The pressure is parallel to the axis of the shaft and the end of the shaft rests on the bearing surface</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="foot-step-bearing">Foot step bearing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foot-step-bearing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Lower end of the vertical shaft is supported by foot step bearing i.e. the shaft is terminated at the bearing [fig. 13.1.4].</li>
<li>A cast-iron block (with a sole), into which a gun-metal bush having a collar at the top is fitted. The bottom end of the shaft rests on a concave steel disc.</li>
<li>A pin is inserted through the body and away from the centre to prevent the disc from rotating. The snug is provided at its neck just below the collar to prevent the bush from rotating.</li>
<li>The collar of the bush is made hollow to serve as an oil cup for lubrication.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="collar-bearing">Collar bearing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#collar-bearing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The pressure is parallel to the axis of the shaft which is passed and extended through the bearing.</p>
<p>In a collar bearing, the shaft continues through and beyond the bearing.</p>
<p>The shaft may be vertical or horizontal with single collar or multi collars. The collars are either integral parts of the shaft or rigidly fastened to it.</p>
<p>Such a bearing is used when it is not practicable to use the foot step bearing.</p>
<p>The collar rotates against the stationary split bearing surfaces.</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-62357d2e.jpeg" alt="img-263.jpeg">
Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-d8aaf75b.jpeg" alt="img-264.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-637cc44f.jpeg" alt="img-265.jpeg">
Fig.13.1.3 Pedestal bearing</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-41d0a122.jpeg" alt="img-266.jpeg">
Engineering Drawing</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-6f31c36d.jpeg" alt="img-267.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251222-9609e664.jpeg" alt="img-268.jpeg">
Fig.13.1 A Foot-step bearing</p>
<p><a href="http://www.AgriMoon.Com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.AgriMoon.Com</a></p>
